[GITA:261] FOREWORD
[GITA:263] The Bhagavad Gita, in spite of being an episode in The Mahabharata,
[GITA:264] has more to it. The Mahabharata, which is said to date back to 5000 B.C., is
[GITA:265] still adored and read, not only for its being an epic of enormous proportions
[GITA:266] but also because it is a tale reflecting the man of all times. If one fee ls that
[GITA:267] it is a fictitious story, it has all that a masterpiece demands. If it is
[GITA:268] considered something beyond and a real tale, it evidences the existence of
[GITA:269] superior human beings.  All the characters in the epic  were highly learned
[GITA:270] and were disciples of Gur u Drona, according to the story. They were all
[GITA:271] human beings, despite the fact that they had the personal interference of
[GITA:272] God himself in their lives.
[GITA:273] This story is an example of the weaknesses that human beings are
[GITA:274] subject to. Arjuna’s doubts in the Bhagava d Gita are the doubts of every
[GITA:275] man. Like when we are in the line of duty, we are often pulled by fears,
[GITA:276] doubts, jealousies, weaknesses, nepotism, etc. Arjuna too was vexed of war
[GITA:277] by his excessive love, respect, and devotion for his elders, relatives, and
[GITA:278] friends whom he had to fight against.
[GITA:279] Here, Arjuna says that he was ‘Nara’ in every respect. He was a bundle
[GITA:280] of human weaknesses, despite being physically strong. Krishna’s sermon to
[GITA:281] Arjuna in the Bhagavad Gita was an attempt to uplift him from his mental
[GITA:282] discrepancy. In other words, it is a great treatise on ‘personality
[GITA:283] development’, a modern buzz word. All the planes of the human mind find
[GITA:284] mention here. It seeks to address all the ills of man, be they physical,
[GITA:285] psychological, spiritual, or whatever.
[GITA:286] And Arjuna here appears to be a studious disciple, with his coming out
[GITA:287] with a great many questions, which rather tickle every learned mind. The
[GITA:288] occasion here was that the Bhagwan himself was the fount of the sermon.
[GITA:289] Hence Arjuna, the archer that he was here fo r the moment, was aiming
[GITA:290] arrows of questions, and those questions were turning out to be real sources
[GITA:291] of light for all mankind, by the divine grace of Bhagwan Krishna. Arjuna,
[GITA:292] in fact, had received instructions from Guru Drona and could never be in
[GITA:293] need of  an elaborate instruction. It was only a momentary delusion for
[GITA:305] xjkhh
[GITA:307] Arjuna, and Krishna arranged that pretext as the stage for giving him the
[GITA:308] sermon of the ‘Bhagavad Gita’. Such human weaknesses are all we are
[GITA:309] prone to. Hence, ‘Bhagavad Gita’ is for all of us.
[GITA:310] Arjuna was sermonised by Lord Krishna in the middle of the battle. It
[GITA:311] was one such occasion where sometimes even great and illumined souls
[GITA:312] could turn ignorant and weak. The Bhagavad Gita’ was an attempt to alert
[GITA:313] Arjuna to human acts on a big stage like th e Kurukshetra battle. It was like
[GITA:314] learning while doing or like a good lesson that we usually learn at the
[GITA:315] height of misery and peril. No wonder that the Bhagavad Gita is a
[GITA:316] prescribed text in India and elsewhere for students.
[GITA:317] Several attempts have been made  at various times to explain the spirit
[GITA:318] of the Gita to man. This one attempt by Dr.Giridhar Boray is born out of
[GITA:319] his intention to bring the ‘ Bhagavad Gita’ to the attention of the present
[GITA:320] day younger generation. It is strengthened in the light of its takin g
[GITA:321] inspiration from the Gita Vivruti  by HH Sri Raghavendra Teertha and
[GITA:322] lectures by HH Sri Vidya Sagara Madhava Teertha Swamiji, head of the Sri
[GITA:323] Madhava Teertha Pontificate and Sree Sameerachar of Tambihalli Math.
[GITA:324] The book makes understanding the concepts ea sier by providing a
[GITA:325] summary of all the chapters at the beginning as well as at the end of each
[GITA:326] verse. The book is really a diligent effort offered at the lotus feet of Lord
[GITA:327] Sri Venkateswara in all humility. A complete study of the whole book
[GITA:328] without deleti ng any subtitle is bound to bring to the common man the
[GITA:329] required spiritual knowledge and also immense satisfaction.
[GITA:332] In the Service of Lord Venkateswara
[GITA:335] Executive Officer
[GITA:336] Tirumala Tirupati Devasthanams
[GITA:337] Tirupati.

[GITA:368] Guru Vandana – Obeisance to the Teacher
[GITA:370] I offer my respectful obeisance to His Holiness Sri Vidyasagara
[GITA:371] Madhava Teertha Swamiji, pontiff of Sr i Madhava  Teertha Pontificate in
[GITA:372] Karnataka, India. His Holiness is the 25 th pontiff in the lineage of Sri
[GITA:373] Madhava Teertha, the 14th century ascetic and philosopher who was a direct
[GITA:374] disciple of Sri Madhwacharya, the exponent of the Dvaita ( theistic dualism)
[GITA:375] school of Indian philosophy.  Vishnu Shastri, a great scholar in the Ad vaita
[GITA:376] (theistic monism) school of philosophy of the 13th century came in contact
[GITA:377] with Sri Madhwacharya , who was reviving the theistic dualism philosophy
[GITA:378] built on the fundamental premise  of a clear difference between the
[GITA:379] individual soul and the Lord Almighty (the Supreme Soul). Vishnu Shastri
[GITA:380] was greatly influenced by the divine, scholarly arguments of Sri
[GITA:381] Madhwacharya and in particular by his commentary on 40 selected hymns
[GITA:382] of the Rig Veda.  He became a disciple of Sri Madhwacharya and entered
[GITA:383] the monastery as Sri Madhava Teertha . He headed the Madhwa Pontificate
[GITA:384] in the 14th century. He is credited to be the only scholar to have composed a
[GITA:385] rigorous commentary on all the four Vedas based o n the concepts of the
[GITA:386] Dvaita philosophy. He was blessed and gifted with the beautiful idol of
[GITA:387] Lord Sri Veera Rama by Sri Madhwacharya , and this idol continues to be
[GITA:388] worshipped to this day by the reigning pontiff.
[GITA:390] The reigning pontiff HH Sri Vidyasagara M adhava Teertha was born
[GITA:391] on the most auspicious Ananta Chaturdashi  day of 1932 to very pious
[GITA:392] parents, in the illustrious lineage of HH Sri Kambaluru Ramachandra
[GITA:393] Teertha of the glorious Sri Vyasaraja Math . He was named Venkatesha. He
[GITA:394] was groomed into every a spect of Sri Madhwa ’s philosophy from his
[GITA:395] childhood days by his father who was also a great scholar. He had a
[GITA:396] brilliant academic record, securing a master’s degree in Literature followed
[GITA:397] by a Doctorate in the Mahabharata from Karnataka University . He c hose
[GITA:398] teaching as his career and served as Professor  and Principal of Government
[GITA:399] Arts College in the state of Andhra Pradesh.
[GITA:412] xiii
[GITA:417] After retiring from active service, he was invited to be the Principal
[GITA:418] of Sri Guru Sarvabhowma Samskruta Vidyapeeta at Mantralayam whe re he
[GITA:419] served at the divine feet of Sri Raghavendra Swamy. Pleased by his service,
[GITA:420] within a year, Sri Raghavendra Swamy chose him to  lead the pontificate of
[GITA:421] Sri Madhava Teertha Moola Samsthana mat h, resulting in an invitation to
[GITA:422] him by the then pontiff Sri Prasanna Shoora Madhavateertha . He  was
[GITA:423] named Sri Vidyasagara Madhavateertha, and he has been serving the
[GITA:424] pontificate for over 19 years performing daily worship to the most sacred
[GITA:425] Sri Veera Rama idol with great devotion and dedication. An erudite scholar
[GITA:426] with proficiency in many aspects of literature, arts, and mastery over many
[GITA:427] languages, HH has composed many devotional songs in Kannada which
[GITA:428] bring out his emotional relationship with the Lord Almighty. HH observes
[GITA:429] strict austerities for ascetics as laid dow n by Sri Madhwacharya and is an
[GITA:430] ideal seer in every sense. Under his stewardship the pontificate has  made
[GITA:431] great strides and attracted many loyal, committed followers.
[GITA:433] Due to divine providence, I got the opportunity to attend the daily
[GITA:434] lectures conducted by His Holiness on Gita Vivruti - a commentary on the
[GITA:435] Bhagavad Gita  by the 16 th century ascetic and philosopher Sri
[GITA:436] Raghavendra Teertha , which is based on the commentary by Sri
[GITA:437] Madhwacharya and Sri Jaya Teertha.  HH graciously supported and blessed
[GITA:438] my effort  (a feeble though!) to capture in English, the essence of Sri
[GITA:439] Raghavendra Teertha’s Gita Vivruti  and his lectures which were in
[GITA:440] Kannada, to benefit a larger audience. I respectfully offer this work to His
[GITA:441] Holiness as Guru Dakshina and seek his blessings.

[GITA:582] Introduction                                                                                                                                              1
[GITA:585] Introduction - Background and Battleground
[GITA:587] Over centuries, the Bhagavad Gita  has been considered an
[GITA:588] important spiritual guidebook and has influenced many thought leaders in
[GITA:589] human history. T he Gita is a conversational poe m and is part of the great
[GITA:590] Indian epic the Mahabharata, which is a story about the families of two
[GITA:591] brothers who inherit a kingdom from their father. These cousins are known
[GITA:592] as the ‘Pandavas’ and the ‘Kauravas’. The Kaurava brothers are 100 in
[GITA:593] number and the Pandavas are 5. The Kauravas, no t wanting to share the
[GITA:594] kingdom with their cousins , invite them to a game of d ice. They use
[GITA:595] dubious means to defeat the Pandavas and annex their kingdom after
[GITA:596] winning the wager. As per the rules of the wager, the Pandavas complete
[GITA:597] their stay outside the kin gdom and come back to ask for their fair share of
[GITA:598] the kingdom so that they can rule with dignity. The Kauravas refuse to
[GITA:599] honor the agreement and even deny the Pandavas a settlement of 5 villages
[GITA:600] so that they can live in peace. As the Kau ravas refuse to bud ge even an
[GITA:601] inch, the Pandavas have no choice but to declare war on them to get back
[GITA:602] their share of the kingdom. During this period, The Lord who ha s
[GITA:603] incarnated as Krishna offers the cousins a choice between His army and
[GITA:604] Himself. But He makes it clear that He Himself would not be participating
[GITA:605] in the war. The Kauravas choose His army as Krishna would not be
[GITA:606] fighting, and the Pandavas are happy to just have Krishna on their side.
[GITA:607] Krishna takes on the role of charioteer to Arjuna’s chariot (Arjuna is the
[GITA:608] third brother among the five Pandavas).
[GITA:610] Before the war starts, the two armies assemble on the battlefield,
[GITA:611] facing each other. At this point, Arjuna requests Krishna to place his
[GITA:612] chariot in the middle so that he can get a full view of the warring forces.
[GITA:613] Since this is a war between cousins, Arjuna sees  his cousins, uncles,
[GITA:614] grandfathers, great grandfathers, nephews, friends, classmates etc. on both
[GITA:615] sides. He is overcome with emotion and grief at the prospect of bloodshed
[GITA:616] among his family and friends for the sak e of a kingdom.  He declares his
[GITA:617] intention to renounce the war and let the Kauravas keep the kingdom, thus
[GITA:629] 2                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:630] abdicating his responsibility as a prince and a warrior. It is at this stage that
[GITA:631] Krishna teaches him the Gita which is structured as a series of ques tions
[GITA:632] from Arjuna and answers  from Krishna.  Krishna extols  the virtue  of
[GITA:633] performing one’s duties regardless of the outcome , and not avoiding
[GITA:634] prescribed duties which may be  difficult and unpleasant. As Krishna starts
[GITA:635] to talk to Arjuna on the virtues of doin g one’s work (in this case Arjuna
[GITA:636] needing to fight a rightful war), Arjuna asks a series of questions on the
[GITA:637] bigger issues of life, individual souls, the Lord Almighty, the universe, the
[GITA:638] circle of life etc., for which Krishna provide s clear, unambiguous an swers.
[GITA:639] He finally convinces Arjuna that his fears were unfound ed and that he
[GITA:640] should fight to free the Kingdom from the Kauravas and provide a just and
[GITA:641] compassionate administration to his citizens.
[GITA:643] At first a battleground is hardly the place for someone to  be
[GITA:644] preaching philosophy.  However, many of the questions that we have in life
[GITA:645] are about the choices we have to make, especially in challenging times. It is
[GITA:646] interesting to note that Sanskrit verses lend themselves to multiple
[GITA:647] meanings and in one interpreta tion, the battleground in the Gita is
[GITA:648] compared to the human body (and mind), and the battle between the
[GITA:649] Pandavas and the Kauravas is compared to the constant strife between good
[GITA:650] and evil thoughts that we encounter daily. The Gita is indeed a teaching for
[GITA:651] all of us , with Arjuna acting as an example of an individual at crossroads,
[GITA:652] desperately looking for guidance and support.
[GITA:654] The Gita is one of the most widely read and commented upon
[GITA:655] spiritual works in human history. There are several excellent books on it in
[GITA:656] various languages. Given that the original work is in Sanskrit, most of the
[GITA:657] scholarly commentaries have been in Sanskrit or other Indian languages
[GITA:658] which are closer to Sanskrit. To read and comprehend many of them, one
[GITA:659] would need some training in Indian s piritual studies , as many of them
[GITA:660] liberally use Sanskrit words albeit transliterated in English. It was felt that
[GITA:661] there is a need to present the key concepts of The Gita in plain English for
[GITA:662] everyday folk , minimizing the use of  Sanskrit words. These observ ations
[GITA:663] led to the development of this manuscript.
[GITA:675] Introduction                                                                                                                                              3
[GITA:678] While there are many learned commentaries on the Gita written over
[GITA:679] the centuries, this author has been greatly influenced by the authoritative
[GITA:680] commentary and lucid explanations of difficult concepts by Sr i
[GITA:681] Madhwacharya, the 12th century ascetic who propounded the philosophy of
[GITA:682] dualistic theism (concept of difference between the almighty Lord and
[GITA:683] individual souls at all times and places). Sri Madhwacharya’s commentary
[GITA:684] has been further elaborated and summari zed by later day savants such as
[GITA:685] Sri Padmanabha Teertha (13 th Century), Sri Jaya Teertha (13th Century) Sri
[GITA:686] Vadiraja Teertha (16 th Century), Sri Raghavendra Teertha (17th century)
[GITA:687] and others.
[GITA:689] The Gita consists of 18 chapters with a total of 70 11 verses in
[GITA:690] Sanskrit. The Gita is part of the great Hindu epic The Mahabharata and
[GITA:691] appears as Chapters 25 to 42, in the Book of Bheeshma in the
[GITA:692] Mahabharata. Most of the verses appear in the anushtap meter, where
[GITA:693] each verse has a total of 32 letters  with 8 words in e ach of the four
[GITA:694] quadrants. Some verses in the 15 th chapter appear in trishtup meter with 44
[GITA:695] words. As per Sri Raghavendra Teertha [GV], t he 18 chapters can be
[GITA:696] broadly classified into 3 sections with 6 chapters in each section. The first
[GITA:697] section with chapters 1-6 (280 verses) broadly outlines how one can  obtain
[GITA:698] divine, spiritual knowledge (jnana upaya), the second section with chapters
[GITA:699] 7-12 (209 verses) focuses on devotion and divine, spiritual knowledge
[GITA:700] (vijnana) that leads one to liberation , and the third  section consisting of
[GITA:701] chapters 13 -18 (212 verses) elaborates on the concepts in the first two
[GITA:702] sections. It is also believed that the Gita conveys at least 10 different
[GITA:703] meanings, all of which are consistent with one another. An illustration of
[GITA:704] multiple meanings in the Gita will appear in Chapter 2.  It is also said that
[GITA:705] the Gita has 3 major interpretations – historical, psychological, and
[GITA:706] spiritual.
[GITA:710] 1 Some texts of Gita indicate a total of 700 verses (omitting the first verse attributed to Arjuna in Chapter
[GITA:711] 13)
[GITA:723] 4                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:724] The Gita and the Number 18
[GITA:726] Numbers and numerology play an incredibly significant  role in
[GITA:727] Indian theology. In fact, the study of knowledge is termed ‘ Sankhya Yoga’,
[GITA:728] with Sankhya meaning ‘arithmetic’. The world around us is based on
[GITA:729] numbers. We are constantly measuring, managing, quantifying all our
[GITA:730] activities with the aid of numbers. We wake up at a certain tim e, we
[GITA:731] work/study for certain hours, we travel a certain distance, we consume
[GITA:732] some amount of food, we transact with certain currency, we perform
[GITA:733] activities at a certain time, on a certain day, in a certain month, in a certain
[GITA:734] year etc. And all these numbers  and calculations are based on the position
[GITA:735] and movement of celestial objects such as the Sun, Moon, stars etc.
[GITA:737] The number 18 is very special in the Gita. Note that every letter has a
[GITA:738] numerical equivalent based on the position of that letter in the Sanskrit
[GITA:739] alphabet which has 16 vowels, 25 consonants and 10 semi vowels. Let us
[GITA:740] review the numerical equivalent for the word ‘ jaya’ (meaning victory)
[GITA:741] which is made up of the letters ja (consonant in the 8 th position) and ya (1st
[GITA:742] semi vowel). Applying the grammat ical rule of reading the numerical
[GITA:743] representations in reverse order, the word ‘ jaya’ is represented by number
[GITA:744] 18. Jaya which means victory , signifies the triumph of good over evil as
[GITA:745] depicted in the Gita and its parent epic, The Mahabharata. Now let us
[GITA:746] review the significance of number 18 in the Gita with the following facts
[GITA:747] [SBG]-
[GITA:749] 1. The Gita has 18 chapters.
[GITA:750] 2. The Mahabharata of which the Gita is a part, has 18 volumes.
[GITA:751] 3. The great war of  the Mahabharata which was the backdrop for the
[GITA:752] Gita was fought over 18 days.
[GITA:754] 4. The armies consisted of 18 regiments with the Kauravas at 11 and
[GITA:755] the Pandavas at 7
[GITA:767] Introduction                                                                                                                                              5
[GITA:769] 5. Each regiment consisted of 21870 elephants, 21870 chariots, 65610
[GITA:770] horses and 109350 soldiers. Each of these numbers sum up to 18.
[GITA:771] For example, in case of 21870, 2+1+8+7+0 = 18
[GITA:773] 6. Duryodhana in his review of the Pandava army  names 18 main
[GITA:774] leaders
[GITA:776] 7. Sanjaya names the 18 conches sounded by the 18 Pandava leaders.
[GITA:778] 8. In the Gita chapter 10, the Lord describes His divine manifestation
[GITA:779] (Vibhuti Roopa) in 18 verses (10.21 to 10.38)
[GITA:781] 9. The Lord Almighty’s omnipresence in the universe is described via
[GITA:782] His 18 forms starting with Hamsa, in Katha Upanishad  (2.2.2),
[GITA:783] which is the basis for many concepts in the Gita.
[GITA:785] Overview of Gita Chapters
[GITA:787] The Gita has 18 chapters, each of which focusses on a specific topic
[GITA:788] and at the same time build s upon the topics covered in previous chapters.
[GITA:789] An enumeration of the chapters and a brief description of the contents of
[GITA:790] each chapter are as follows:
[GITA:792] Section 1: The Path to Divine Knowledge, Chapters 1 – 6 (280 verses)
[GITA:794] Chapter 1 (47 verses) - Arjuna Vishada Yoga  (Arjuna’s Distress): This
[GITA:795] chapter sets the stage for the Gita. The two armies of cousins, the
[GITA:796] Pandavas and  the Kauravas were assembled in Kurukshetra , ready to
[GITA:797] start the war. The proceedings in the wa r were narrated by Sanjaya, an
[GITA:798] aide to the blind king Dhrutarashtra, father of the Kaurava brothers.
[GITA:799] Before the start of the war, Arjuna looks at both the armies and realizes
[GITA:800] that on both sides were many of his near and dear ones such as teachers,
[GITA:801] cousins, uncles, nephews etc.  He develops cold feet at the thought of
[GITA:802] bloodshed between them. He wonders if he is doing the right thing by
[GITA:803] waging this war as it would not only lead to bloodshed but also result in
[GITA:815] 6                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:816] societal degradation. He advances many arguments as  to why he should
[GITA:817] avoid this war and find some other way to lead his life. At the end of this
[GITA:818] chapter, Arjuna who is grief stricken , is seen sitting still in his chariot
[GITA:819] after dropping his weapons, confused as to the way forward.
[GITA:821] Chapter 2  (72 verses) - Sankhya Yoga  (The Path of Doctrines): In this
[GITA:822] chapter, the Lord answers Arjuna’s basic questions about the purpose of
[GITA:823] life and related philosophical concepts. Sensing that Arjuna’s
[GITA:824] reservations about fighting the war were due to his fear of losing his
[GITA:825] loved ones, the Lord delves into the nature of individual souls, the
[GITA:826] indestructibility of souls, the relationship between individual souls and
[GITA:827] the Lord Almighty etc., to remind Arjuna that the Lord gave human
[GITA:828] forms to individual souls to help them in their spir itual journey , and
[GITA:829] everyone must play their dutiful role in society. The Lord urges Arjuna
[GITA:830] not to despair over the hurt that may be caused to his loved ones in the
[GITA:831] war, as they had taken the side of the evil king Duryodhana . Arjuna had
[GITA:832] an obligation to fig ht the just war to protect his citizens. Lord Krishna
[GITA:833] teaches Arjuna to focus on performing his prescribed duty of protecting
[GITA:834] his citizens as an offering to Him and not let his em otions come in the
[GITA:835] way. Chapter 2 can be considered  a summary of the entire Gita as it
[GITA:836] answers Arjuna’s (and through Arjuna, everyone’s) basic questions
[GITA:837] about the purpose of life. The remaining chapters serve to expand on the
[GITA:838] concepts presented in this chapter.
[GITA:840] Chapter 3  (43 verses) - Karma Yoga  (The Path of Action): In this
[GITA:841] chapter, the Lord answers Arjuna’s questions regarding the paths of duty
[GITA:842] (karma) and knowledge ( jnana). Arjuna wonder s if there is a way, he
[GITA:843] could focus on acquiring knowledge and traverse  the path of knowledge
[GITA:844] (jnana) avoiding this dreadful war. The Lord logically answers Arjuna’s
[GITA:845] doubts to convince him that everyone has an obligation to perform their
[GITA:846] duties to society and as a prince, it was his responsibility to fight the just
[GITA:847] war to protect his citizens. To perform one’s duty, one also needs to be
[GITA:848] equipped with knowledge and in that sense, one needs to incorporate
[GITA:849] both karma and jnana in one’s spiritual journey. Based on one’s innate
[GITA:861] Introduction                                                                                                                                              7
[GITA:863] nature, some may be prescribed a path that is dominant in action , and
[GITA:864] others may be prescribed a path dominant in knowledge. This chapter
[GITA:865] also has an excellent discussion on the Cyclic Order of the Universe.
[GITA:867] Chapter 4 (42 verses) - Jnana Yoga (Wisdom in Action ): This chapter is
[GITA:868] predominantly about the spiritual knowledge that a seeker will need to
[GITA:869] perform prescribed action ( karma). Knowledge regarding the individual
[GITA:870] souls, various deities and the attributes of  the Lord are explained to
[GITA:871] instill devotion in a seeker. The Lord explains that He incarnates in this
[GITA:872] world from time to time to establish virtu e and vanquish immorality.
[GITA:873] Knowledge about the  various types of act ions – right action, wrong
[GITA:874] action and non-action are also covered here.
[GITA:876] Chapter 5  (29 verses) - Sanyasa Yoga  (The Path of Renunciation) :
[GITA:877] Renunciation of rewards from actions is termed sanyasa and performing
[GITA:878] prescribed duties as an offering to the Lord is termed yoga (action). In
[GITA:879] this chapter, the Lord answers Arjuna’s questions on some fundamental
[GITA:880] aspects of the two concepts of sanyasa and yoga. Arjuna’s argument was
[GITA:881] that if renunciation were a better option tha n action, he c ould perhaps
[GITA:882] adopt the life of an ascetic and avoid waging the  war. The Lord however
[GITA:883] describes the characteristics of a renunciant and clarifies that one must
[GITA:884] incorporate both these paths to achieve self -realization. The Lord
[GITA:885] reiterates that Arjuna  must focus on performing his prescribed duty of
[GITA:886] waging war to protect his citizens from  the evil Kauravas. This chapter
[GITA:887] also delves into the importance of detachment and self-control.
[GITA:889] Chapter 6  (47 verses) – Atma Samyama  Yoga (The Path of Self
[GITA:890] Restraint) or Dhyana Yoga  (The Path of Meditation) : Yoga (or action)
[GITA:891] has two components – external and internal. The external component
[GITA:892] refers to actions performed as part of one’s prescribed duties which
[GITA:893] would be visible to others. The internal component refers to dhyana (or
[GITA:894] meditation). This chapter has a detailed discussion of dhyana yoga .
[GITA:895] Characteristics of a true yogi are described. The physical aspect of
[GITA:896] meditation such as place , physical pose, breath control etc., are also
[GITA:908] 8                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:909] described. Requirements for moderatio n in consumption are also
[GITA:910] discussed. To a question from Arjuna regarding the condition of those
[GITA:911] who make a sincere attempt but are not able to successfully implement
[GITA:912] karma and dhyana, the Lord teaches that no sincere effort is a waste , and
[GITA:913] such seekers wil l get additional opportunities to complete their spiritual
[GITA:914] journey.
[GITA:916] Section 2: Divine, Spiritual Knowledge, Chapters 7 – 12 (209 verses)
[GITA:918] Chapter 7  (30 verses) - Jnana Vijnana Yoga  (The Path of Knowledge
[GITA:919] and Wisdom ): The first 6 chapters were broadly conc erned with
[GITA:920] descriptions o f the methods and procedures (both external and internal)
[GITA:921] to acquire divine, spiritual knowledge. The next 6 chapters describe in
[GITA:922] more detail such divine, spiritual knowledge about the Lord Almighty ,
[GITA:923] which forms the principle messa ge of the Gita. In earlier chapters 2 – 6,
[GITA:924] there were some discussions on the Lord’s attributes which were
[GITA:925] included contextually, but the next 6 chapters provide more exclusive
[GITA:926] details about the Lord’ attributes. Chapter 7 provides a detailed
[GITA:927] discussion of  the two types of knowledge – indirect (ordinary
[GITA:928] knowledge) and direct  (special knowledge or wisdom). Indirect
[GITA:929] knowledge ( jnana) is that knowledge that is gained from scriptural
[GITA:930] studies and through teachers. Direct knowledge ( vijnana) is specialized
[GITA:931] knowledge about the Lord that one obtains after self -realization. The
[GITA:932] Lord describes His ownership of everything in the universe and stresses
[GITA:933] that He is responsible for even the intrinsic attributes of objects.  The
[GITA:934] causes of ignorance, the influence of maya (delusion) on living beings ,
[GITA:935] and procedures to overcome maya are also discussed.  The various types
[GITA:936] of seekers who are devoted to the Lord are identified.  This chapter ends
[GITA:937] with the Lord stating that one who has proper knowledge about
[GITA:938] Adhiyajna (Lord of Sacri fice) and Adhyatma (Prime Self),  along with
[GITA:939] that of Adhibhoota (Prime Material) and Adhidaiva (Prime Deities), and
[GITA:940] who thinks of Him during the last moments will eventually reach Him.
[GITA:953] Introduction                                                                                                                                              9
[GITA:955] Chapter 8 (28 verses) - Akshara Brahma Yoga  (The Imperishable Lord) :
[GITA:956] This chapter starts with Arjuna asking Krishna 8 questions about the
[GITA:957] topics indicated towards the end of the previous chapter . Arjuna also
[GITA:958] enquires about the need for and advantages of remembering the Lord
[GITA:959] during one’s last moments. This chapter also details the journey of a soul
[GITA:960] after leaving the physical body.
[GITA:962] Chapter 9 (34 verses) - Raja Vidya, Raja Guhya Yoga (Path of Supreme
[GITA:963] Knowledge and Supreme Secrets): This chapter continues with the
[GITA:964] description of some of the important and subtle attributes and act ivities
[GITA:965] of the Lord. In that sense , it is Supreme knowledge that is taught here.
[GITA:966] The Lord uses the term ‘ secretive’ to arouse curiosity in Arjuna (and
[GITA:967] everyone else). While the Lord has infinite auspicious forms, attributes,
[GITA:968] activities etc., there are eigh t activities of  the Lord that directly impact
[GITA:969] living beings. They are – creation ( Srishti), sustenance ( Stithi),
[GITA:970] dissolution (laya), regulation ( niayamana), knowledge ( jnana),
[GITA:971] ignorance (ajnana), bondage (bandha) and liberation (moksha). Together
[GITA:972] they are called Janmadi ashtaka  and they are also covered in this
[GITA:973] chapter.
[GITA:975] Chapter 10 (42 verses) - Vibhooti Yoga (Divine Manifestations) : No one
[GITA:976] can fully comprehend the Lord’s infinite, auspicious attributes. The
[GITA:977] Lord, who is responsible for the creation, suste nance and dissolution of
[GITA:978] the universe, powers everyone and everything. The Lord answers
[GITA:979] Arjuna’s questions about His manifestations and presence in superior
[GITA:980] entities. The Lord’s special presence among both sentient beings (such as
[GITA:981] various deities, animals)  and inert objects (such as mountains, rivers,
[GITA:982] stars, weapons) bestows superiority on these entities. Divine
[GITA:983] manifestations are of two types – direct, where the Lord incarnates in
[GITA:984] different forms such as Rama, Krishna, Vedavyasa etc., and intrinsic,
[GITA:985] where the Lord’s special presence in some objects gives them superiority
[GITA:986] over other objects (ex: Meru’s superiority among mountains, Ganges’s
[GITA:987] superiority among rivers).
[GITA:1000] 10                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1001] Chapter 11  (55 verses)  - Vishwa Roopa Darshana m (The Lord’s
[GITA:1002] Universal Form) : Arjuna, after l earning about the Lord’s innumerable
[GITA:1003] manifestations, requests the Lord to  allow him to see His Universal form
[GITA:1004] and is granted divine vision by Him. Arjuna sees the Lord having the
[GITA:1005] brightness of thousands of suns, pervading the entire world with infinite
[GITA:1006] limbs, faces with no beginning or end  etc. The Lord’s infinite forms are
[GITA:1007] as real as the universe. This chapter also has a soulful prayer by Arjuna
[GITA:1008] on seeing the Lord’s universal form. While the Lord had previously
[GITA:1009] taught Arjuna about His greatness and attrib utes, the act of physically
[GITA:1010] seeing the Lord’s universal form completely solidifies Arjuna’s faith in
[GITA:1011] Sri Krishna’s teachings.
[GITA:1013] Chapter 12 (20 verses) - Bhakti Yoga (The Path of Devotion) : The Lord
[GITA:1014] answers Arjuna’s questions comparing the worship of Goddess
[GITA:1015] Mahalakshmi and that of the Lord Almighty Narayana. Worship ping
[GITA:1016] Lord Narayana is an easier way to attain moksha, while pleasing
[GITA:1017] Mahalakshmi is difficult. Further, Sri Lakshmi operates under the Lord’s
[GITA:1018] control. One should propitiate Sri Lakshmi and other deities that are part
[GITA:1019] of the Lord’s entourage for maximum effect. Bhakti or devotion is
[GITA:1020] focusing one’s mind on the Lord. The Lord teaches other ways of
[GITA:1021] devotion using facilities such as intellect, studying scriptures, performing
[GITA:1022] prescribed duties etc. The l ast 8 verses describe the characteristics of an
[GITA:1023] ideal devotee who is dear to the Lord.
[GITA:1025] Section 3: Reiteration of Concepts in Sections 1 and 2, Chapters 13 – 18
[GITA:1026] (212 verses)
[GITA:1028] Chapter 13  (35 verses)  - Kshetra Kshetrajna Yoga  (The Field and the
[GITA:1029] Knower of the  Field): The f irst section cover s the tools and utilities to
[GITA:1030] acquire Divine knowledge which itself was covered in detail in the
[GITA:1031] second section. This third section expands on some of the critical issues
[GITA:1032] briefly covered in the first two sections. In chapter 7, verse (7.4), the
[GITA:1033] Lord briefly speaks about eight-fold nature which operate s under His
[GITA:1034] command and control. This chapter details that field or theatre of
[GITA:1046] Introduction                                                                                                                                              11
[GITA:1048] operations and the one who knows them.  The interaction between the
[GITA:1049] components of nature (sentient an d inert) and living beings are also
[GITA:1050] discussed.
[GITA:1052] Chapter 14  (27 verses) - Gunatraya Vibhaga Yoga  (Division of
[GITA:1053] Qualities): This chapter has details on the three qualities of nature (sattv
[GITA:1054] – piety, rajas – passion, tamas – ignorance) which define one’s
[GITA:1055] personality. These concepts were introduced in chapter 3 (3.5) and are
[GITA:1056] expanded here. Creation of the universe through sentient Mother Nature
[GITA:1057] is described. The ways in which the three qualities bind a person are also
[GITA:1058] explained. The methods to escape from the clutches of the three qualities
[GITA:1059] are described.
[GITA:1061] Chapter 15 (20 verses) - Purushottama Yoga (Theology of the Supreme
[GITA:1062] Being): This chapter is considered by many to have the core teachings of
[GITA:1063] the Gita. The Bhagavad Gita has 700 verses spread among 18 chapters .
[GITA:1064] Interestingly, this chapter which has 20 verses has 700 letters  (15 verses
[GITA:1065] have 32 letters each and the remaining 5 verses have 44 letters  each)!
[GITA:1066] This gives credence to this chapter’s importance based on numerology.
[GITA:1067] While the Lord had previously described the components of the universe
[GITA:1068] such as living beings, inert objects, and the Lord Almighty, in this
[GITA:1069] chapter the Lord illustrates the interrelations among them using the
[GITA:1070] example of a banyan tree.  In verse 15, the Lord explicitly states His
[GITA:1071] presence in every one and declares that He is the primary subject matter
[GITA:1072] of the Vedas. In verses 16 -20, the Lord emphatically describes Himself
[GITA:1073] as the Supreme Being intrinsically different from all other beings,
[GITA:1074] thereby convincingly establishing the philosophy of dualis m ( dvaita)
[GITA:1075] which was propagated by Sri Madhwacharya.
[GITA:1077] Chapter 16 (24 verses) - Daivasura Sampat Vibh aga Yoga (The Divine
[GITA:1078] and the Demoniac Nature): This chapter delves deep into the types of
[GITA:1079] human nature. In the previous chapters, there were many references to
[GITA:1080] pious and virtuous people with divine nature and to people with
[GITA:1081] demoniac nature. This chapter provides more details on human traits.
[GITA:1093] 12                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1094] These traits typically are intrinsic to an individual. One cannot judge
[GITA:1095] people based on external appearance, position in so ciety etc. The
[GITA:1096] personality traits of p eople with divine nature and those with demoniac
[GITA:1097] nature are described in detail.
[GITA:1099] Chapter 17  (28 verses)  - Shraddhatraya Vibhaga Yoga  (The Threefold
[GITA:1100] Faith): In the previous chapters , especially chapter 14 , the nature o f
[GITA:1101] individuals influenced by the qualities of sattv, rajas, tamas were stated
[GITA:1102] a number of  times. This chapter has more details on the concept of
[GITA:1103] threefold faith with detailed discussion on the impact of these qualities
[GITA:1104] on various issues such as faith, food habits, sacrificial rituals, charitable
[GITA:1105] giving, meditation etc.
[GITA:1107] Chapter 18  (78 verses)  - Mokshasanyasa Yoga  (Liberation and
[GITA:1108] Renunciation): This chapter starts with a discussion on the subtle
[GITA:1109] differences between renunciation ( sanyasa) and relinquishment of  the
[GITA:1110] rewards of labour ( tyaga). The types of rewards from actions are also
[GITA:1111] discussed. The five prerequisites required for performing acti vity are
[GITA:1112] identified. Different types of knowledge, action, doers, intellect,
[GITA:1113] determination, and happiness are also disc ussed. Additional details on
[GITA:1114] the four societal categories of people are provided (this was briefly
[GITA:1115] indicated in (4.13) ). The concept of liberation ( moksha) is discussed in
[GITA:1116] detail in this chapter. The first 55 verses contain a discussion on the
[GITA:1117] topics ident ified so far. The Lord concludes His sermon in the next 17
[GITA:1118] verses. Arjuna responds to Lord Krishna’s teachings in verse 73.
[GITA:1119] Sanjaya, who has been describing the proceedings of the war and the
[GITA:1120] Lord’s glorious expounding of dharma (The Gita) to Arjuna ,
[GITA:1121] euphorically concludes his commentary in the following 5 verses.
[GITA:1123] Organization of the Book
[GITA:1125] This book is primarily based on the interpretation of the Gita by Sri
[GITA:1126] Raghavendra Swamy (Sri Rayaru) in his pioneering work, Gita Vivruti .
[GITA:1127] Vivruti means ‘explanation’ an d in  the Gita Vivruti , Sri Rayaru has
[GITA:1128] consolidated unique interpretations on the Gita by Sri Madhwacharya (in
[GITA:1140] Introduction                                                                                                                                              13
[GITA:1142] his works Gita Bhashya, Gita Tatparya Nirnaya ) and commentaries on
[GITA:1143] these two works by Sri Jaya Teertha  (Prameya Deepika  and Nyaya
[GITA:1144] Deepika). Gita B hashya is the very first work of Sri Madhwacharya,
[GITA:1145] where he provided  a unique interpretation of the Gita. In a subsequent
[GITA:1146] work, Gita Tatparya Nirnaya , Sri Madhwacharya provides detailed
[GITA:1147] arguments and the scriptural basis for many of the interpretations of Gita
[GITA:1148] Bhashya.
[GITA:1150] This book has 18 main chapters with each chapter corresponding to
[GITA:1151] the relevant chapter in the Gita. In each chapter, the book presents the
[GITA:1152] original Sanskrit verse along with a simple translation in English. At the
[GITA:1153] end of the original text, a n explanation of key concepts covered in the
[GITA:1154] chapter is provided , based on authentic commentaries on the Gita by Sri
[GITA:1155] Raghavendra Teertha (Sri Rayaru) in his work Gita Vivruti and on lectures
[GITA:1156] by Sri Vidyasagara Madhava Teertha . At the end of each chapter, a
[GITA:1157] summary of that chapter is provided. The summary section also refers to
[GITA:1158] relevant verses in that chapter  for clarity , and this section can be read
[GITA:1159] ‘stand-alone’ by those readers who want a quick summary of the various
[GITA:1160] chapters. It is hoped that the readers will find this version useful in better
[GITA:1161] understanding the greatest psychological manual ever written – The
[GITA:1162] Bhagavad Gita.
[GITA:1164] Sri Vishnu Teertha from Madinur, a great saint  philosopher of the
[GITA:1165] 18th century has identified one verse from each chapter of the Gita as a
[GITA:1166] representation of the core tenets of that entire chapter  in his work
[GITA:1167] Bhagavad Gita Saroddhara . This was done to help devotees who wanted
[GITA:1168] to chant the Gita everyday but could not chant all 700 verses. These core
[GITA:1169] verses are highlighted in this book in italics.
[GITA:1171] This book has 3 annexures. The first annexure contains a section
[GITA:1172] from the epic Varaha Purana which describes the glory of The Gita and
[GITA:1173] is typically read after completing a reading of the Gita. The second
[GITA:1174] annexure has a discussion on the philoso phical interpretation of the Gita
[GITA:1175] with a sample illustration. The third annexure is about the core tenets of the
[GITA:1187] 14                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1188] philosophy of Sri Madhwacharya and the corresponding verses in the Gita
[GITA:1189] that support these tenets.
[GITA:1191] The last section of this book identifies l iterature cited in this book.
[GITA:1192] The principal references are the Gita, part of  the great epic the
[GITA:1193] Mahabharata and commentaries on the  Gita by Sri Madhwacharya, Sri
[GITA:1194] Jaya Teertha and Sri Raghavendra Teertha. The references are indicated by
[GITA:1195] their initials withi n square brackets. For example, the Gita Bhashya  is
[GITA:1196] identified as [GB].

[GITA:1219] Benedictory Verse  (Gita B hashya) : Salutations to  the Lord and
[GITA:1220] teacher Narayana (Vedavyasa) who is revealed by the Gita. He is ever
[GITA:1221] devoid of defects such as dependence and ignorance. He is not limited by
[GITA:1222] time, space, and attributes. (Even though the Gita is full of many meanings,
[GITA:1223] only a simple meaning is told here to aid general readers).

[GITA:1227] Benedictory Verse  (Gita Vivruti) : After saluting Lord Narayana
[GITA:1228] (consort of Lakshmi ), Sri Madhwacharya,  and other teachers , we are
[GITA:1229] summarizing the concepts from Gita Bhashya, Gita Tatparya Nirnaya
[GITA:1230] and related commentaries which offer a unique perspective on the
[GITA:1231] Bhagavad Gita taught by Lord Sri Krishna.
[GITA:1232] Chapter Introduction: Sri Vedavyasa composed the great epic
[GITA:1233] Mahabharata which contain s all the essential teachings from the Vedas,
[GITA:1234] and this allows even those who do not have access or eligibility to learn the
[GITA:1235] Vedas to gain necessary knowledge to help them in their spiritual journey.
[GITA:1236] The epic Mahabharata is spread over 18 volumes and the Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1237] (or Gita, in short), is part of Bheeshma Parva (or the Book of Bheeshma).
[GITA:1238] The Mahabharata is compared to the divine flower parijata, and the Gita
[GITA:1239] is like the nectar (or essence)  of the flower. Sri Raghavendra Teertha (Sri
[GITA:1240] Rayaru) has composed a unique commentary on the Gita titled, Gita
[GITA:1241] Vivruti [GV] which is a summary of the two commentaries on  the Gita by
[GITA:1253] 16                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1254] Sri Madhwacharya namely, Gita Bhashya  [GB] and Gita Tatparya
[GITA:1255] Nirnaya [GTN]. The Gita has 70 1 verses spread over  18 chapters. Sri
[GITA:1256] Rayaru has organized these 18 chapters into 3 sections of 6 chapters each .
[GITA:1257] The first se ction (chapters 1 -6) broadly describe s the proce sses and
[GITA:1258] prerequisites required to achieve divine, spiritual knowledge that one would
[GITA:1259] need in one’s spi ritual journey. The second section (chapters 7 -12)
[GITA:1260] describes the nature of the divine, spiritual knowledge and the third se ction
[GITA:1261] (chapters 13-18) expands on some of the key concepts covered in sections 1
[GITA:1262] and 2.
[GITA:1263] The Bhagavad Gita is a conversation between A rjuna, and Lord
[GITA:1264] Krishna as narrated by Lord Vedavyasa in the epic, Mahabharata. The
[GITA:1265] great war between the armies of the Kaurava and  Pandava cousins, was
[GITA:1266] witnessed by Sanjaya, the aide of the blind King Dhrutarashtra who was
[GITA:1267] head of the Kaurava dynasty. Sanjaya rushes to Dhrutarashtra soon after the
[GITA:1268] fall of Bheeshma, the commander of the Kaurava army  [Mahabharata,
[GITA:1269] Bheeshma Parva, Chapter 13] . The stunned Dhrutarashtra wants to know
[GITA:1270] everything that happened on the battlefield from the very beginning.
[GITA:1271] Sanjaya narrates all the events from the beginning of the war. He stays with
[GITA:1272] the king till the end of the war relaying all the events to him. He can do this
[GITA:1273] because Sage Vedavyasa grants him divine vision to remotely view the war
[GITA:1274] and provide him with updates.
[GITA:1275] All 47 verses in the first chapter and the first 10 verses in the second
[GITA:1276] chapter provide the background and context for Arjuna’s apprehensions
[GITA:1277] about the war, and Lord Krishna starts His sermon to Arjuna then onwards.
[GITA:1278] Dhrutarashtra starts by asking the following question to Sanjaya.
[GITA:1279] What did my Clan and the Pandavas do in Kurukshetra?

[GITA:1296] Chapter 1                                                                                                                                              17
[GITA:1298] Dhrutarashtra said:  O Sanjaya! Assembled at Kuru Kshetra, the
[GITA:1299] designated land f or committing righteous acts ( Field of Righteousness),
[GITA:1300] what did my clan of Kauravas and Pandavas do? (1.1)
[GITA:1302] Comments: The first verse in the Gita starts with King
[GITA:1303] Dhrutarashtra asking the above question to his aide Sanjaya. The first word
[GITA:1304] here is ‘Dharma’ which is one of the names of the Lord Almighty who
[GITA:1305] shoulders (dharana) the universe. In that sense , the first verse also acts as a
[GITA:1306] benedictory verse of the Gita.
[GITA:1308] The King begins by asking Sanjaya what happened between his clan
[GITA:1309] (Kauravas) and the Pandavas in Kurukshetra which was known as Dharma
[GITA:1310] Kshetra or the ‘Field of Righteousness ’. This question reveals the King’s
[GITA:1311] apprehensions. He was convinced that  the Pandavas with the guidance of
[GITA:1312] Lord Krishna would be invincible (but Krishna had sworn not to take up
[GITA:1313] arms). On the other hand,  Bheeshma was also known to be invincible and
[GITA:1314] so the King hoped against hope that his sons would not be defeated in this
[GITA:1315] war that was being fought in an area known as the ‘Field of Righteousness’.
[GITA:1316] Hence, he was surprised to hear that Bheeshma had fallen.
[GITA:1318] Sri Madinur Vishnu Teertha Swamiji in his work Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1319] Saroddhara [BGS] identifies this verse as the central piece of chapter 1.
[GITA:1320] After all, chapter 1 sets the context for the entire Gita, and it starts with
[GITA:1321] King Dhrutarasht ra’s question. The theatre of war is our body , and the
[GITA:1322] armies represent the angelic and demoniac forces inside each of us. The
[GITA:1323] interaction between them is the context for the Gita.

[GITA:1343] 18                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1344] Sanjaya said:  King Duryodhana , upon seeing the formation of the
[GITA:1345] Pandavas’ army, immediately rushed to  his teacher Drona, and said as
[GITA:1346] follows. (1.2)
[GITA:1348] Comments: Sanjaya answers Dhrutarashtra’s question in the
[GITA:1349] following 9 verses. Sri Sumateendra Teertha in his Bhava Ratna Kosha
[GITA:1350] [BRK] draws attention to the use of the word ‘ tu’ as implying
[GITA:1351] Duryodhana’s anxiety as soon as he saw the formidable Pandava army (‘tu’
[GITA:1352] is normally used to add emphasis). Hence, the Kaurava prince immediately
[GITA:1353] rushes to  his teacher Drona.  It is interesting to note that Duryodhana
[GITA:1354] approaches his teacher Drona and not the commander of the Kaurava
[GITA:1355] forces, Bheeshma,  to give him inf ormation about the Pandava army ,
[GITA:1356] perhaps to acknowledge the fact that many warriors in  the Pandava army
[GITA:1357] were Drona’s students.

[GITA:1362] O My Teacher! Look at the formation of the mighty army of
[GITA:1363] Pandavas which has been organized by your cle ver student
[GITA:1364] Dhrushtadyumna, son of Drupada. (1.3)
[GITA:1366] Comments: Duryodhana reminds Drona that the formation of the
[GITA:1367] Pandava army has been organized by Dhrushtadyumna, a  brilliant warrior
[GITA:1368] who learnt the art of warfare from Drona  himself, implying that  the
[GITA:1369] Pandavas have a formidable army formation. Duryodhana also refers to the
[GITA:1370] Pandavas’ army as mighty, even though his own army was much bigger.

[GITA:1394] Possessing heavy weapons are the brave warriors Yuyudhana
[GITA:1395] (Satyaki), Virata, and King Drupada, all Maharathas. They are as capa ble
[GITA:1396] as Bheema and Arjuna. (1.4)
[GITA:1398] Other brave warriors include Drushtaketu, Chekitana, Kashiraja who
[GITA:1399] are extraordinarily strong , Purujit, Kuntibhoja and Shybhya who are
[GITA:1400] superior among men. (1.5)
[GITA:1402] Yudhamanyu, the brave warrior, Uttamowja , the strong warrior,
[GITA:1403] Abhimanyu, son of Subhadra, and sons of Draupadi are also here. They too
[GITA:1404] are Maharathas. (1.6)
[GITA:1406] Comments:  Duryodhana recognizes and names 18 leaders from the
[GITA:1407] Pandava army whom he considers as being comparable to Bheema and
[GITA:1408] Arjuna in valor. They are – Dhrushtadyumna, Satyaki, Virata, Drupada,
[GITA:1409] Drushtaketu, Chekitana, Kashiraja, Purujit, Kuntibhoja, Shybhya,
[GITA:1410] Yudhamanyu, Uttamowja, Abhimanyu and the five sons of Draupadi
[GITA:1411] (Parativindya, Sutasoma, Shrutakeerti, Shataneeka, Shrutakarma). These
[GITA:1412] leaders were termed  ‘Maharathas’, meaning they were capable of single -
[GITA:1413] handedly fighting 10,000 enemy soldiers. They were experts in the use of
[GITA:1414] both types of weapons – astra and shastra. Here, astra refers to weapons
[GITA:1415] such as arrows that are launched after praying and invoking  a deity , and
[GITA:1416] shastra refers to arrows that are launched without such invocation.
[GITA:1418] Interestingly, Duryodhana does not name Krishna who was
[GITA:1419] charioteering Arjuna. This is perhaps due to Krishna’s vow that He would
[GITA:1420] not take up arms in the war. Duryodhana prob ably thought Krishna d id not
[GITA:1421] count if He was not fighting. A big miscalculation!

[GITA:1445] O Drona! Superior among the wise men! Now I will enumerate the
[GITA:1446] great warriors from our side. So, please listen with attention (1.7)
[GITA:1448] Our side includes yourself, Bheeshma, Karna, Krupa who has won
[GITA:1449] many wars, Ashwatthama, Vikarna and Bhoorishravas, son of Somadatta.
[GITA:1450] (1.8)
[GITA:1452] Along with them are many warriors ready to sacrifice their lives for
[GITA:1453] my sake. They are all well versed in wars and are armed with heavy
[GITA:1454] weapons. (1.9)
[GITA:1456] Comments: Duryodhana now names 7 leaders from his side. He
[GITA:1457] indicates that they are all ready to sacrifice their lives for him. Duryodhana
[GITA:1458] is looking at this war purely to justify holding onto  the Pandavas’ share of
[GITA:1459] the kingdom and believes that everyone in his side is fighting to please him
[GITA:1460] and not for any higher cause. It is interesting to note that while Duryodhana
[GITA:1461] names 18 leaders from the Pandavas, he only rattles 7 names from his side.
[GITA:1462] Further he does not name his brothers such as Dushasana. This points to
[GITA:1463] some doubts in his mind about his brothers’ capabilities.

[GITA:1481] Chapter 1                                                                                                                                              21
[GITA:1483] Comments: After reviewing the two armies, Duryodhana is
[GITA:1484] beginning to have doubts about the capabilities of his army. He knows that
[GITA:1485] his is not a just cause, but as a representation of Kali, the evil soul, it is in
[GITA:1486] his nature to adamantly get into a war even when he knows that he is wrong
[GITA:1487] and that his side is not capable of winning. Note that while
[GITA:1488] Dhrushtadyumna is the commander of the Pandavas’ army, Duryodhana
[GITA:1489] recognizes that the real leader of  the Pandavas is the hugely experienced
[GITA:1490] Bheema.

[GITA:1534] Conch-shells, bugles, trumpets, drums, and cow horns were sounded
[GITA:1535] loudly by the Kauravas which result ed in their sound filling the entire
[GITA:1536] space. (1.13)
[GITA:1538] Comments: Taking a cue from Bheeshma, other Kaurava leaders
[GITA:1539] sounded various organs to signal their readiness and command their troops
[GITA:1540] to get ready. It is interesting to note that while Bheeshma sounds his  conch,
[GITA:1541] a musical instrument with spiritual significance which has a pleasant sound,
[GITA:1542] other leaders sound different instruments, many making harsh sounds not in
[GITA:1543] harmony with each other. Perhaps an indication of disharmony and disunity
[GITA:1544] among the Kauravas.

[GITA:1549] At this time, Lord Madhava (Krishna) and Arjuna, son of Pandu,
[GITA:1550] who were seated on a magnificent chariot pulled by white horses, sounded
[GITA:1551] their respective divine conch shells. (1.14)
[GITA:1553] Comments: Lord Krishna and Arjuna sounding their conches while
[GITA:1554] seated in a horse drawn chariot also has a spiritual interpretation. As noted
[GITA:1555] by Sri Vidyamanya Teertha in his GPM, as per the Upanishads, the chariot
[GITA:1556] is a representation of the human body. The wandering horses represent the
[GITA:1557] sense organs, and the road travelled represents the objects of sense
[GITA:1558] pleasure. The charioteer (Krishna) represents the intellect , and the leash
[GITA:1559] which controls the horses represents the mind. The charioteer (Krishna) and
[GITA:1560] the passenger (Arjuna) respectively represent the Supreme Soul and the
[GITA:1561] individual soul resident in each body. The conch is shaped like the
[GITA:1573] Chapter 1                                                                                                                                              23
[GITA:1575] primordial symbol ‘Om’ which is the root letter of all scriptures. Hence the
[GITA:1576] conversation between Krishna an d Arjuna represents the collective wisdom
[GITA:1577] of scriptures such as the Vedas.

[GITA:1709] 26                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:1710] Sanjaya said: O Bhaarata! ( Dhrutarashtra, from the lineage of King
[GITA:1711] Bharata)! Lord Hrishikesha (Krishna), addressed thus by Gudakesha
[GITA:1712] (Arjuna) positioned the grand chariot in between the two armies. (1.24)
[GITA:1713] Turning the chariot towards Bheesh ma, Drona and other kings, Lord
[GITA:1714] Krishna said - O Partha (Arjuna)! Look at all the Kauravas assembled here.
[GITA:1715] (1.25)
[GITA:1716] Comments: Arjuna is referred to as Gudakesha – the one who has
[GITA:1717] conquered sleep ( gudaka). Sleep , along with lethargy represents tamas
[GITA:1718] (ignorance). Sleep is probably the most difficult state for one to overcome.
[GITA:1719] Thus, anyone who can control sleep is said to have control over sense
[GITA:1720] organs. Hence, Arjuna is most eligible to receive the sermon from the Lord.
[GITA:1721] The Lord is referred to as Hrishikesha, on e who has control over
[GITA:1722] everyone’s sense organs and indeed on everyone and everything in the
[GITA:1723] universe.
[GITA:1725] VÌmní`V² pñWVmZ² nmW©… {nV¥ZW {nVm_hmZ² Ÿ&
[GITA:1726] AmMm`m©Z² _mVwbmZ² ^«mV¦Z² nwÌmZ² nm¡ÌmZ² gItñVWm Ÿ&
[GITA:1727] œewamZ² gwöXü¡d goZ`moé^`moa{n Ÿ&& 26 &&
[GITA:1729] VmZ² g_rú` g H$m¡ÝVo`… gdm©Z² ~ÝYyZdpñWVmZ² Ÿ&
[GITA:1730] H¥$n`m na`m@@{dï>mo {dfrX{ÞX_~«drV² Ÿ&&27&&
[GITA:1732] Looking at both the armies, Arjuna sees warriors who were like his
[GITA:1733] father (father figures) , father -in-law, grandfathers, uncles, teachers,
[GITA:1734] brothers, sons, grandsons, friends, and colleagues. (1.26)
[GITA:1735] Seeing all his relatives assembled there to wage the war, Arjuna (son
[GITA:1736] of Kunti) was overwhelmed by emotion and addresse d Sri Krishna with
[GITA:1737] great anxiety. (1.27)
[GITA:1738] Comments: While Arjuna knew that many of his near and dear ones
[GITA:1739] would be par ticipating in the war, he is overwhelmed when he sees all of
[GITA:1751] Chapter 1                                                                                                                                              27
[GITA:1753] them assembled in one place. Faced with the prospect of many of them
[GITA:1754] dying, regardless of who w on the war, Arjuna is overcome with grief and
[GITA:1755] emotion.
[GITA:1757] Sri Rayaru in GV  provides an explanation for  the term vishada
[GITA:1758] which is used to express Arjuna’s emotion. Vishada means mental
[GITA:1759] weakness caused by grief, which is a result of delusion , which in turn is
[GITA:1760] caused by the fear of losing loved ones.
[GITA:1762] Arjuna Develops Cold Feet
[GITA:1764] AOw©Z CdmM Ÿ&
[GITA:1765] ÔîQ‰>o_§ ñdOZ§ H¥$îU `w`wËgw§ g_wnpñWV_² Ÿ&& 28 &&

[GITA:1768] dodWwü earao _o amo_hf©ü Om`Vo Ÿ&& 29 &&
[GITA:1770] Arjuna said:  O Krishna! I am looking at my relatives and friends
[GITA:1771] gathered here to wage the war. (1.28)
[GITA:1773] Looking at them, my limbs are weakenin g, my face is crumpling, my
[GITA:1774] body is trembling, and my hair is bristling. (1.29)
[GITA:1776] Comments: From this verse, till the end of this chapter is a
[GITA:1777] description of Arjuna’s anxiety and fear when faced with the prospect of
[GITA:1778] waging the war which would result in the loss of lives of multitudes of
[GITA:1779] people including many of his near and dear ones. Note that Arjuna begins
[GITA:1780] describing his anxiety to the Lord by addressing Him as Krishna. This has a
[GITA:1781] special significance. Sri Madhwacharya in his Gita Bhashya  [GB] (5th
[GITA:1782] Chapter), interprets Krishna as ‘ Lokarakshaka’, the one who controls
[GITA:1783] everyone and everything in the universe. By using this term, Arjuna

[GITA:2025] Chapter 1                                                                                                                                              33
[GITA:2027] As will be seen later, Arjuna fights the war and  the Pandavas
[GITA:2028] successfully rule their kingdom for many years. None of Arjuna’s fears
[GITA:2029] come true. The evil clan of the Kauravas and their supporters such as the
[GITA:2030] virtuous but guilty Bheeshma and Drona are vanquished, and society does
[GITA:2031] not fall apart.  In fact, Bharat enjoys a long, peaceful and prosperous period
[GITA:2032] under Pandavas’ rule. The Gita clearly suggests that omens do not have
[GITA:2033] any predictive value and  chastise superstitious practi ces. Arjuna’s
[GITA:2034] experience of bad omens reflect his weak mental condition which is caused
[GITA:2035] by his excessive attachment to his near and dear ones.
[GITA:2037] Arjuna Drops His Weapons

[GITA:2043] Sanjaya said: Having said thus, Arjuna , stricken with deep sorrow in
[GITA:2044] his mind , drops his bow and arrows and sits still in the middle of his
[GITA:2045] chariot. (1.47)
[GITA:2047] Comments: While Arjuna indicates to Krishna his anxiety and
[GITA:2048] unwillingness to wage war,  his relinquishing of  his bow and arrows is a
[GITA:2049] signal to his large army that he is not yet ready to start the war . (The army
[GITA:2050] of course  could not have heard the conversation between Arjuna and
[GITA:2051] Krishna). This action has le d to the ir enemies drawing their own
[GITA:2052] conclusions which Krishna indicates later.
[GITA:2054] Summary of Chapter 1
[GITA:2056] The Bhagavad Gita  is a conversation between Arjuna  and Lord
[GITA:2057] Krishna as narrated by Lord Vedavyasa in the epic, Mahabharata. The
[GITA:2058] great war between the armies of the Kaurava and the  Pandava cousins, was
[GITA:2059] witnessed by Sanjaya, aide of the blind King Dhrutarashtra who was head
[GITA:2060] of the Kaurava dynasty. Sanjaya rushes to Dhrutarashtra soon after the fall
[GITA:2072] 34                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:2073] of Bheeshma, the commander of the Kaurava army. The stunned
[GITA:2074] Dhrutarashtra wants to know everythin g that happened on the battlefield
[GITA:2075] from the very beginning. Sanjaya narrates all the events from the beginning
[GITA:2076] of the war. He stays with the king till the end of the war relaying all the
[GITA:2077] events to him. He can do this because Sage Vedavyasa grants him divin e
[GITA:2078] vision to view the war remotely and provide Dhrutarashtra the updates.
[GITA:2079] The King starts by asking Sanjaya as to what happened between his
[GITA:2080] clan (the Kauravas) and the Pandavas in Kurukshetra (which is known as
[GITA:2081] Dharma Kshetra or the ‘ Field of Righteousness ’) (1.1). This question
[GITA:2082] reveals the King’s apprehensions.  He is convinced  that the Pandavas with
[GITA:2083] the guidance of Lord Krishna would be invincible (but, Krishna has sworn
[GITA:2084] not to touch or take up arms ).  On the other hand, Bheeshma, the very
[GITA:2085] epitome of justi ce, was also  known to be invincible. The King  was
[GITA:2086] therefore hoping  against hope that his sons would not be defeated ,
[GITA:2087] especially in a war that was being fought in an area known as the Field of
[GITA:2088] Righteousness. So, he was shocked to  hear that Bheeshma had fallen.
[GITA:2089] Sanjaya responds , saying that initially Duryodhana looks at the Pandava
[GITA:2090] army and rushes to inform his teacher Dronacharya about its strength. He
[GITA:2091] makes sure to let him know that the army is being led by Dhrishtadyumna ,
[GITA:2092] a student of Drona himself . Then h e goes on to enumerate other great
[GITA:2093] leaders on the side of the Pandavas. These leaders were termed
[GITA:2094] ‘Maharathas’, meaning they were capable of single-handed fighting with
[GITA:2095] 10,000 enemy soldiers. In all, Duryodhana enumerates 18 leaders from the
[GITA:2096] Pandavas’ side, other than the five Pandava brothers. Interestingly, he does
[GITA:2097] not recognize Krishna as being a part of the Pandava side. This is because
[GITA:2098] Krishna had vowed not to use any arms in the war. Duryodhana obviously
[GITA:2099] did not realize the invaluable advice and guida nce that Krishna would be
[GITA:2100] providing to Arjuna. Ultimately, this miscalculation had lead to the
[GITA:2101] Kauravas’ defeat. This illustrates the peril of ignoring the Lord who is
[GITA:2102] responsible for everything in this universe , just because one cannot
[GITA:2103] physically see Him. (1.2-1.6). Duryodhana then enumerates the great
[GITA:2104] warriors on his side which included – Dronacharya, Bheeshma, Karna,
[GITA:2105] Krupa, Ashwatthama, Vikarna and Bhoorishravas. However, he did not
[GITA:2106] expect the Pandavas to turn up with a huge, well-organized army. He
[GITA:2118] Chapter 1                                                                                                                                              35
[GITA:2120] knows that in addition to the experienced warriors, the Pandava army also
[GITA:2121] included the 5 young children of Draupadi and each of them was brave and
[GITA:2122] capable. He  also knows  that while Dhrushtadyumna is the commander of
[GITA:2123] the Pandava army, it is really the invincibl e Bheema who is their star
[GITA:2124] warrior. Duryodhana is now clearly very perturbed. He wants to ensure that
[GITA:2125] Bheeshma is well protected as he sees Bheeshma as extremely critical to
[GITA:2126] his victory. He asks Dronacharya and others to ensure Bheeshma’s safety
[GITA:2127] (1.7-1.11). At this time, sensing Duryodhana’s worry, Bheeshma decided to
[GITA:2128] instil confidence in him by loudly roaring like a lion and sounding his
[GITA:2129] conch. Other soldiers in the Kaurava army follow by sounding drums,
[GITA:2130] trumpets, bugles etc, and the collective sound seemed to envelope the entire
[GITA:2131] space. Now it is the turn of the Pandavas. Sri Krishna and Arjuna , while
[GITA:2132] seated in their chariots sound their respective conches followed by other
[GITA:2133] leaders in their army. The collective sound of these conches reverberated
[GITA:2134] across ear th and sky and seem ed to shatter the hearts of King
[GITA:2135] Dhrutarashtra’s sons. It is interesting to note that while the Kauravas used
[GITA:2136] various instruments to sound their readiness, the Pandavas uniformly
[GITA:2137] sounded their respective conches for  the maximum effect. L ord Krishna
[GITA:2138] and Arjuna sounding their conches while seated in a horse drawn chariot
[GITA:2139] also has a spiritual interpretation. The chariot is a representation of the
[GITA:2140] human body as noted in the Upanishads. The horses which pull the chariot
[GITA:2141] represent the sense org ans, and the road s travelled represent  objects of
[GITA:2142] sense pleasure. The charioteer (Krishna) represent s the intellect , and the
[GITA:2143] leash which controls the horses represent s the mind. The charioteer
[GITA:2144] (Krishna) and the passenger (Arjuna) represent the Supreme soul  and the
[GITA:2145] individual soul resident in each body. The conch is shaped like the
[GITA:2146] primordial symbol ‘Om’ which is the root letter of all scriptures. Hence the
[GITA:2147] conversation between Krishna and Arjuna represent s the collective wisdom
[GITA:2148] of all the scriptures such as the Vedas (1.12-1.19). Arjuna requests Krishna
[GITA:2149] to take the chariot to the middle of the battleground so that he can have a
[GITA:2150] good look at both the armies. He recognizes his teachers, cousins, nephews,
[GITA:2151] uncles, grand uncles, children, friends etc., and gets very depressed. He is
[GITA:2152] distressed at the very thought of waging a war where he himself might kill
[GITA:2153] many of his near and dear ones. Arjuna tells Krishna that his body is
[GITA:2165] 36                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:2166] trembling, and  that he is unable to hold his weapons . He was feeling
[GITA:2167] disoriented and disil lusioned. He was further seeing bad omens and felt
[GITA:2168] there was nothing good coming out of this war. He says he does not crave
[GITA:2169] for victory, or a kingdom won after losing so many near and dear ones
[GITA:2170] (1.20-1.39). He reasons that with massive destruction of men, familial
[GITA:2171] values would perish, and unrighteousness would take over society. Arjuna
[GITA:2172] says that such a war has no benefits either here or hereafter. He says he
[GITA:2173] would rather drop his weapons and let  the Kauravas kill him rather than be
[GITA:2174] responsible for such a de structive war. Saying thus, Arjuna drops his
[GITA:2175] weapon to the ground and sits in his chariot feeling extremely dejected
[GITA:2176] (1.40-1.47).
[GITA:2178] The rest of the Gita is a sermon from Lord Krishna to Arjuna to help
[GITA:2179] him overcome his distress and doubts. The dropping of weapons by Arjuna
[GITA:2180] is equivalent to one abdicating one’s responsibility. At this stage, the
[GITA:2181] following points are worth noting: Duryodhana was also distressed after
[GITA:2182] looking at the Pandavas’ army but there is a big difference between the
[GITA:2183] distress of Duryodhana and that of Arjuna. Duryodhana was distressed at
[GITA:2184] the thought of not being able to win the war and thereby usurp  the
[GITA:2185] Pandavas share of the kingdom  illegally. On the other hand, Arjuna’s
[GITA:2186] distress was out of compassion towards his loved ones. Duryodhana shows
[GITA:2187] no such compassion. Arju na was not afraid of fighting as he had fought
[GITA:2188] many wars before and  had won them. But this time it was different. His
[GITA:2189] enemies were people he cared for dearly.
[GITA:2191] && B{V àW_mo@Ü¶m`… &&
[GITA:2192] End of Chapter 1
[GITA:2204] Chapter 2                                                                                                                                      37
[GITA:2207] {ÛVr`mo@Úm`… - gm§»¶¶moJ…
[GITA:2208] Chapter 2 – The Path of Doctrines
[GITA:2210] Chapter Introduction: This chapter of 72 verses is termed Sankhya
[GITA:2211] Yoga (The Path of Doctrines) . In this chapter, the Lord answers Arjuna’s
[GITA:2212] basic questions about the purpose of life and other related philosophical
[GITA:2213] concepts. S ensing that Arjuna’s reservations about fighting the war were
[GITA:2214] due to his fear of losing his loved ones, the Lord delves into the nature of
[GITA:2215] individual souls, the indestructibility of souls, the relationship between
[GITA:2216] individual souls and  Himself.  He  reminds Arjuna that He gave human
[GITA:2217] forms to individual souls to help them in their spiritual journey and that
[GITA:2218] everyone must play their duti ful role in society. In verses 2.12 to 2.39 , the
[GITA:2219] Lord lectures on the nature of individual souls and the Supreme soul (Lord
[GITA:2220] Almighty). In verse 2.39 , the Lord tells Arjuna how to acquire divine,
[GITA:2221] spiritual knowledge which is explained in brief in verses 2.45 to 2.51 . In
[GITA:2222] these verses, the Lord discusses the concept of nishkama karma , that is ,
[GITA:2223] performing prescribed duties without ex pectations.  The Lord urges Arjuna
[GITA:2224] not to despair over the hurt that may be caused to his elders and teachers in
[GITA:2225] the war, as they had chosen to support  the evil king Duryodhana and
[GITA:2226] reminds Arjuna that he had an obligation to fight th is just war to protect his
[GITA:2227] citizens. Lord Krishna teaches Arjuna to focus on performing his
[GITA:2228] prescribed duty of protecting his citizens as an offering to the Lord
[GITA:2229] Almighty and not let his emotions come in the way. Chapter 2 can be
[GITA:2230] considered the foundational chapter  of the entire  Gita as it answers
[GITA:2231] Arjuna’s (and through Arjuna, everyone’s) basic questions about the
[GITA:2232] purpose of life. The remaining chapters serve to expand on the concepts
[GITA:2233] presented in this chapter.
[GITA:2235] g§O` CdmMŸ -
[GITA:2236] V§ VWm H¥$n`m@@{dï>‘² AlwnyUm©Hw$bojU_² Ÿ&
[GITA:2237] {dfrXÝV{_X§ dmŠ`_² CdmM _YwgyXZ… Ÿ&& 1 Ÿ&&
[GITA:2250] 38                                                                                                                             The Bhagavad Gita
[GITA:2251] Sanjaya said:  Lord Madhusoodana said the following to grief-
[GITA:2252] stricken Arjuna who was overcome with self -pity and had tears in his eyes.
[GITA:2253] (2.1)
[GITA:2255] Comments: Sensing Dhrutarashtra’s curiosity as to the events that
[GITA:2256] follow Arjuna’s dropping of his weapons, Sanjaya continues with his
[GITA:2257] narration of the next set of events starting with Lord Krishna’s questioning
[GITA:2258] of Arjuna’s actions.
[GITA:2260] Arjuna, Rise Up and Wage this Just War
[GITA:2262] lr^JdmZwdmM Ÿ&
[GITA:2263] Hw$VñËdm H$í_b{_X§ {df_o g‘wnpñWV_² Ÿ&
[GITA:2264] AZm`©Owï>_ñd½`©_² AH$s{V©H$a_Ow©Z Ÿ&& 2 Ÿ&&
[GITA:2266] The Lord said:  O Arjuna! What caused this delusion for you to give
[GITA:2267] up your duties at the time of crisis? This is not expected of wise men, it
[GITA:2268] does not lead to glory, or heavens and it only brings disrepute. (2.2)

[GITA:2440] The Lord said:  You are grieving over those who do not deserve

[GITA:2445] Comments: Elders and teachers such as Bheeshma and Drona, even

[GITA:2452] Z Ëdodmh§ OmVw Zmg§ Z Ëd§ Zo_o OZm{Ynm… Ÿ&

[GITA:2455] There was no time when neither Me nor yo u nor any of these kings
[GITA:2456] here did not exist. Even in future there will be no time when any of us will
[GITA:2457] cease to exist. (2.12)
[GITA:2459] Comments: Krishna goes on to answer three conflicts in Arjuna’s
[GITA:2460] mind which are causing him distress. Is the distress due to a fear  of the loss
[GITA:2461] of souls of loved ones? Or is it due to a fear of the loss of their physical
[GITA:2462] bodies? Or is it due to a fear of the loss of their bodies which are equated to
[GITA:2463] their souls?
[GITA:2465] He answers the first question in this verse where he categorically
[GITA:2466] states that souls are eternal and have no beginning or end. This applies to
[GITA:2478] Chapter 2                                                                                                                                      43
[GITA:2480] Arjuna, to all kings assembled at the warfront (and by extension everyone)
[GITA:2481] and especially to the Lord Almighty, which is indicated using the word ‘tu’.
[GITA:2483] The fact that the Lord is espec ially eternal is supported by scriptures
[GITA:2484] such as Katha Upanishad  (2.5.13) which describes the Lord as ‘ nityo
[GITA:2485] nityanam’ (He is eternal among eternals). While the individual souls are
[GITA:2486] eternal, the Lord’s eternality is superior to that of individual souls. Li ving
[GITA:2487] beings whose souls are eternal do undergo creation and destruction of their
[GITA:2488] physical bodies (birth and death), but the Lord who has a transcendental
[GITA:2489] body does not experience creation or destruction. Hence, He is said to have
[GITA:2490] superior eternality compar ed to individual souls. The other two question s
[GITA:2491] are answered in later verses 2.13 and 2.20.
[GITA:2493] A question that arises at this stage is - how could Arjuna, himself a
[GITA:2494] realized soul as an incarnation of deity Devendra, the king of all deities,
[GITA:2495] harbour such doub ts about the nature of the Lord?  Sri Madhwacharya
[GITA:2496] answers this question  [GTN] by stating that the Lord in the form of
[GITA:2497] Vedavyasa in the great epic Mahabharata elaborates the sermon in more
[GITA:2498] detail for the benefit of humanity.
